preface.txt
Oh, my very dear sons, I have completed for you this book which is the last of the science of medicine, along with its completion, and have made the end through its expositions and its declarations. And it came to me in a vision that I complete it for you with this treatise, which is part of the work of hands, that is to say "surgery".	AFTER finishing for you, my sons, this book which is the part of knowledge dealing with medicine in its entirety; and having made it as clear and explicit as possible, I thought it well to complete it for you by adding this treatise which concerns surgical operating.	0.76893353
And because the work of hands is neglected in our region and is completely foreign, the science is lost and fragmented, and there remains nothing of it except the short descriptions in the books of the ancients which hands have copied. And from this has come error and vanity, and understanding is obscured and knowledge is driven away.	For the skilled practitioner of operative surgery is totally lacking in our land and time; so that the knowledge of it is on the point of being blotted out and its remains lost; and there is nothing left of it except a few traces in the books of the Ancients; where, however, it has been so corrupted by the hands of scribes, and subjected to error and confusion, that its meaning has become obscured and its value diminished.	0.8002136
Now it seems to me that I want to revive it with the organization of this treatise according to the way of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and I will include with it the forms of cautery irons, and of other instruments of the work with the addition of the description and preparation of them, as is necessary.	Therefore I decided to revive this art by expounding, elucidating, and epitomizing it in this treatise; and to present the forms of the cauterizing irons and other operative instruments, since this is an adjunct to explanation and a vital necessity.	0.8017312
And because no good master of hands is found in our time, the art of medicine is diminished. And whoever undertakes it must excel in the science of anatomy, as Galen said, so much that he knows the arrangement of the limbs, and their forms and connections and separations, and has knowledge of the bones and nerves and arms and their number, and the movement of the moving and resting veins, and the place of their origin.	Now this is the reason why there is no skilful operator in our day: the art of medicine is long and it is necessary for its exponent, before he exercises it, to be trained in anatomy as Galen has described it, so that he may be fully acquainted with the uses, forms, and temperament of the limbs; also how they are jointed, and how they may be separated; that he should understand fully also the bones, tendons, and muscles, their numbers and their attachments; and also the bloodvessels, both arteries and veins, with their relations.	0.84099275
And therefore Hippocrates said that the name with the name is much, and with the work is little; and especially in the art of the hand.	And so Hippocrates said: 'Though many are doctors in name, few are in reality, particularly on the surgical side.'	0.6812434
We tell you in the introduction of this book that whoever is not knowledgeable about the things mentioned above, let him beware that he does not fall into error and that he does not kill people; so that I have often seen many of those who meddle in this science and thus abandon themselves without knowledge and without experience.	We have already spoken of this in the introduction to this book. For he who is not skilled in as much anatomy as we have mentioned is bound to fall into error that is destructive of life. Thus I have seen many laying claim to this knowledge and boasting of it, but having neither knowledge nor experience.	0.7771743
For I saw a foolish doctor who cut into a scrofulous abscess in the neck of a woman, and cut the arteries of the neck; so much blood came out that the woman fell dead in his hands.	I saw an ignorant doctor incise a scrofulous tumour in a woman's neck; and he cut certain arteries in the neck so that the woman bled until she fell dead before him.	0.8767668
And I saw another doctor who extracted a stone from an elderly man, and the stone was large; he took with it a piece of the bladder, and the man died within three days.	And I saw another doctor presume to take out a stone from a man of advanced age. The stone was large; he rushed blindly at the task and extracted the stone and with it a piece of the bladder itself; and the man was dead in about three days.	0.93456614
And I had already been called there to extract it, and I saw the condition of the patient and the size of the stone, and I knew well that if he were cut he would die from it.	I had myself been previously called in to extract the stone but from what I saw of the size of it and the condition of the patient I judged that the outcome would be so.	0.6930748
And I saw another doctor who took into his care a duke of our land who had a broken leg, and with the break there was a wound; and the doctor, full of ignorance, tightened the break over the wound with splints by strong binding, and left no opening at all for the wound, and left it like that for a while; and he ordered him not to untie it; and it remained like that until the leg was completely swollen and the foot as well, and it came to ruin.	I saw another doctor who had a regular salary from one of the high officers of our country for medical treatment. There had occurred to a negro boy of his a fracture of the leg near the heel, together with a wound; the doctor rushed in, in his ignorance, and bound up the fracture, over the wound, very tightly, with pads and splints, not allowing the wound to breathe. He then let him go as he wished and then dismissed him for some days bidding him not to loose the bandage. Eventually his leg and foot swelled and he was close to death.	0.8608542
I was called to come to him, and I hastened to come and unbind the leg; and when I had unbound it, he was at peace and felt no pain.	Eventually his leg and foot swelled and he was close to death. I was called in and made haste to loose the bandage, upon which some ease and reduction of the pain followed.	0.6602051
But all the same the infection was already established in the limb, and I could not heal it; and the infection did not stop spreading through the limb until the limb was completely lost.	Nevertheless, gangrene had already taken hold on the limb and I could not hinder its progress; it. continued to extend in the limb until he perished.	0.74175334
And I saw another doctor who lanced a festering abscess, and then it became inflamed through the incompetence of his treatment.	And I saw another doctor incise a malignant tumour; after some days the place broke out in open ulceration, thereby adding to the misery of the patient.	0.7408076
For a canker, when it is full of melancholic humor, should not be touched with iron unless it is in such a place that all that is corrupted can be removed.	For a cancer that arises from a melancholic humour should never be touched by the knife unless it should happen to be in a part of the body which may be removed thoroughly and in its entirety.	0.6729127
And therefore, dear son, you need to know that manual work is divided into two parts: namely, work where there is danger in many forms; and I will show in each place of this book where there is fear and danger.	Wherefore you must know, my sons, that operative surgery is divided into two parts, namely, where operation is associated with the health of the patient, and, on the other hand, where it is for the most part fraught with danger. Throughout the following book I point out wherever I have described an operation that is accompanied by fear and danger.	0.72040546
Galen said in some of his admonishments: "Do not undertake to treat a bad disease, lest you be called a foolish doctor.	For Galen once said in certain of his admonitions: 'Do not treat mischievous maladies lest you get the name of mischievous doctors.'	0.8787497
I have divided this treatise into three chapters.	I have divided this treatise into three books:	0.9002188
The first chapter is about fire cauteries and sharp medicinal cauteries, and the chapter is organized from the head down to the feet; and about the forms of the instruments, and the cautery irons, and everything that is necessary for the work.	BOOK ONE. Concerning the actual cautery; and cauterization by caustics. It is divided into chapters set in order from the head to the feet. The shapes of the instruments are shown and the iron cauteries and all things necessary for operating.	0.7928997
The second chapter is about cutting and piercing, and cupping glasses and wounds, and the extraction of arrows, and similar things; and the chapter is organized with the forms of the instruments.	BOOK TWO. Concerning incision and perforation; blood-letting and cupping; wounds and the extraction of arrows and the like. The whole is divided into chapters in order; and illustrations of instruments are given.	0.8048222
The third chapter is about restoring dislocations and sprains, and similar things; and it is organized from the head down to the feet, and with the forms of the instruments.	BOOK THREE. Concerning the setting of bones; dislocations; the treatment of sprains and the like. This is similarly divided into chapters set in order from head to feet, with illustrations of instruments.	0.76789093

chapter-1.txt
Before we recall the work, it is necessary that I describe the quality of the help and the harm of each complexion.	Before we expatiate on the actual operation of cautery it is necessary to mention its advantages and disadvantages and the temperament upon which it is to be used.	0.55329794
I say that the qualities of help and harm are the longest discourse and subtle science and hidden secret; and many wise men have spoken of this, and agreed on it; and their words suffice little for me, for I fear the length.	I say that a discourse on the advantage and disadvantage of cautery is a long one, a subtle learning, a hidden secret. A great number of wise men have spoken on the subject with differing opinions. Lest I should be over long I have reduced their opinions to a summary.	0.76858133
I say that cautery helps universally every complexion where there is matter, except for two complexions: that is to say, a hot complexion without matter, and a dry complexion without matter.	Now I say that the cautery has universal application for every ill constitution, whether organic or functional, with the exception of two: the functional hot temperament and the functional dry temperament.	0.65502316
In a hot and dry complexion together with matter, some disagreed and said that cautery is a great help there; and others said the opposite: that cautery is not good for an illness that comes from heat and dryness.	About the organic dry temperament the Ancients differed, some saying that cautery helps in a constitution of this kind, while others said the opposite: that cautery is of no use in a disease caused by heat or dryness; for fire is naturally hot and dry and it is obviously absurd to treat a hot dry sickness with a hot dry remedy.	0.8044735
For the nature of fire is hot, and a hot illness should not be treated by heat, nor by dryness; and he who said the opposite said that cautery is a great help for a hot and dry illness that comes into the body of men. For when you compare the body of a man and his moisture to the complexion of fire, you find the body cold.	But he who holds the opposite view says that actual cautery may help in the case of such hot dry illnesses as arise in the human body; for when you compare the human body, humid as it is, with the nature of fire you find the human body cold.	0.74924815
And I agree with this statement. For I have sometimes found it by experiment.	I myself from much experience agree with this last opinion.	0.60294574
And in any case, no one should interfere except those who have experience and who know, by long use, the chapter of cauteries, and the diversity of human complexions, and the dispositions and causes of their illnesses, and the occurrences and the length of time; we have no fear of other complexions, especially with cold and moist illnesses. And all the physicians assembled and agreed on the cautery.	However, no one should attempt this operation unless he has had long training and practice in the use of the cautery, and is fully acquainted with the various human temperaments, and the character of the complaints in themselves; their causes, symptoms, and duration. Concerning the rest of the temperaments there is no reason for fear, particularly the cold and humid distempers; all medical men are agreed and none differs concerning the utility of the cautery in such cases.	0.86489654
And know, son, that cautery with fire is among the secrets of cures, and of greater benefit than cautery with burning medicine.	Now you should know, my sons, that the actual cautery and its superiority over cauterization with chemical caustics is one of the secrets of medicine.	0.70055556
For fire is simple, and does not go beyond the limb that is burned, and does not harm other limbs except a little.	For fire is a simple substance having no action except upon the actual part cauterized; nor does it do more than slightly harm any adjacent part.	0.7784377
Burning medicines spread from one limb to another, and cause illnesses in the limb that are difficult to heal; and perhaps kill the patient. Fire, because of its loftiness and the goodness of its substance, does not do this, unless there is excess.	But the effect of cauterization with caustic may spread to parts at a distance from that burnt; and also in the part cauterized it may give rise to a disease difficult to cure or even fatal. Whereas fire, on the other hand, on account of the nobility of its nature and its superiority has no such effect unless it is overdone.	0.7237612
And this I have found through experiment and the length of service and study that I have put into the art, and it was based on the truth of things; and therefore I excuse myself for the length of the speech, for I do not want to deprive you of the secret that lies in fire, and the quality of the work, how it expels illnesses from the body, as the philosopher says in demonstrating it; now receive it in your thoughts.	That has become clear to us by experience through length of service, devotion to the art, and acquaintance with the facts of the matter. So I need not prolong this discourse. Were it not inappropriate to this my book I should relate to you a deep secret concerning fire, the manner of its action on bodies and its expulsion of diseases; with a reasoned philosophical discourse too subtle for your understanding.	0.7511455
And know, son, that they agreed on what time cauterizations should be done, and they chose spring.	Know also, my sons, that the Ancients disagreed also as to the fit time for cautery, affirming that spring was the best.	0.6595832
And I say that cautery is suitable in all seasons, because the sudden harm is lessened by the help of the cautery; and especially if the cautery is for necessary, sudden and urgent pains that cannot wait for the time when a greater malady might come to the limb.	Myself, I say that the cautery is suitable at all times; for whatever harm may arise from the season of the operation is utterly outdone by the benefit deriving from the cauterization itself; especially if the cautery is applied to pains that are severe, grievous, and swift, brooking no delay, because of the fear that the consequences may be more grave than the slight harm from the season.	0.83916533
And do not ever think, dear son, as the foolish physicians do; for the illness that is healed by cautery does not return; and they say so; but it is not as they think, because cautery is nothing but a medicine that changes the complexion, and dries the humors that are the cause of the pain, and is worth more than medicine because of the speed of its effect, and because of the virtue of its work and its mastery.	Nor let enter your minds, my sons, the groundless notion of the laity and of ignorant doctors, that the complaint cured by the cautery never comes back later; do not cleave to this opinion. The fact is not as they think; for the cautery is homologous to the drug which alters the constitution and dries up the humidities that give rise to disorders. But the cautery excels the drug by the rapidity of its success, the strength of its action, and the potency of its powers.	0.83915305
And it is possible that the illness sometimes comes according to the complexion of the patient and the location of the illness and its nature; and superfluities are gathered in him by the acquisition of foods and similar things, unless cautery is administered in a place where there are few superfluities and moisture, such as cautery of teeth for pain, and similar things, and such pain does not return; but this happens rarely.	It may be that the disease will return at some time according to the temperament of the patient, and the persistence and virulence of the disease; and from the formation of a mass of superfluities in the body; from his neglect of himself in acquiring them from food; and from causes of a similar nature. Of course, if the disease to which the cautery is applied be a slight one, and in a part only slightly liable to superfluities and humidities, as, for example, the cauterization of a molar tooth for toothache, and the like, then it may happen that the pain does not return; but that happens rarely.	0.8844105
And many people do not understand why cautery is called the last of medicines, and it is true; but they do not believe that cautery is useful after medicine, but their understanding is false, and cautery is the last of medicines because when we administer kinds of treatments for some illness and they are not effective, then we administer cautery, and it works. Then it is clear that cautery is the last of medicines, not according to the understanding of the foolish physicians.	For they believe that after cauterization no treatment, either with or without medicine, will help. It means something precisely the opposite: after various treatments have been applied to a certain disorder without success, then last of all we apply cauterization and this proves effective; whence comes about the saying that the cautery is the final remedyâ€”not in that sense in which it is popularly held by the laity and by many ignorant medical men.	0.8270449
The ancients said that cautery with a gold instrument is better than with iron, and they said this only because of the quality of gold and because of the excellence of its substance; and they said that the site of the cautery does not suppurate, but this is not true for I have tested it, and have found that it is true in some men; and gold cautery is better than iron, as they said.	The Ancients have stated that cauterization by gold is more effective than by iron. Now they said that on account of the temperateness of gold and its noble character. They also stated that the site of cauterization does not become purulent, but that is not absolutely so; for I myself have experience of that and have found that it happens in some cases and not in other cases.	0.84799993
And yet when you heat a gold instrument, you do not know when it is hot by the color of the gold, and it cools down quickly; and if it is heated too much, it melts in the fire; and thus there is considerable impediment.	Cauterization by gold is indeed better and more successful than with iron, as they have stated; except that when you are heating the gold cautery in the fire you are uncertain, by reason of the redness of the gold, when it reaches the desired temperature. In addition to this it cools very quickly; and if you overheat it it melts in the fire, melts and runs, and the practitioner finds himself in difficulties.	0.7586317
And for this reason we use iron cautery because we have it better prepared for our work.	Therefore in our own opinion cauterization is swifter and more successful with iron.	0.48999128
Now I have arranged this chapter from head to toe, so that the way is easier to find.	Now I have arranged this book on cauterization by chapters, set in order from the head to the foot, to make it easy for the seeker to find what he wants.	0.7592875

This cautery is good for moisture and coldness on the brain, and there are two that are made for a disease called "soda", and for many humors that descend from the head to the eyes, and to the ears, and for a multitude of dreams and toothache and sore throat, and especially for all diseases that come from coldness such as paralysis and lethargy and apoplexy and similar illnesses.	This cauterization helps against excessive humidity and coldness on the brain, which cause headache, abundance of defluxions from the head to the regions of the eyes and ears, excess of sleep, toothache, sore throat, and in general any sort of disorder arising from frigidity, such as palsy, fits, apoplexy, and diseases similar to these.	0.70099187
The method for this cautery is that the patient be purged by a dissolving medicine cleansing the head for three or four nights, according to what the strength, age and constitution of the patient can bear; and then shave the patient's head.	The manner of performing this operation is first to bid the patient open the bowels with an evacuant which will also clear his head, for three or four nights, according to the strength, age, and habits of the patient.	0.8531958
Then have him sit cross-legged between your hands, and place his hands on his chest; then place the base of your palm on the bridge of his nose between his two eyes; and where your middle finger falls, bleed from there, then heat the cautery which is called "olive-shaped", of which this is the form:	Then tell him to have his head shaved; then seat him cross-legged before you, with his hands on his breast. Then place the lower part of your palm upon the root of his nose between his eyes; and where your middle finger reaches mark that place with ink. Then heat an olivary cautery.	0.83860433
And the ancients said that the longer the wound remains open, the more it is purged; and for this it helps to change dressings.	The Ancients said that the more a wound is kept open, discharging pus, the better and more efficacious.	0.68091893
And some of these said that one should leave the cautery until part of the bone is burned, and then cut away what is burned from the bone, and it should be treated.	Some said the skin was to be cauterized through to the bone and the cautery held until some of the thickness of the bone was burnt. Then the burnt bone was to be scraped out, then dressed.	0.77489734
And others say that one should leave the cautery on, and push so hard that one lowers the bone as wide as a whorl that women put on their spindles so that the humors come out more easily; and one should leave the wound open for a long time; then one should clean and solder it.	Then the burnt bone was to be scraped out, then dressed. Others said that the cautery should be carried through to burn a deep impression in the bone so that there should come out of the bone a piece shaped like a grain of a carob tree or a small spindle-whorl; they asserted that the vapours of the head would be exhaled through this place. The wound should be kept open a long while, then dressed until healed.	0.7219944
And these two ways of cauterizing do not seem to me to be good at all, except in some men where there is a way of fear; and it seems to me that I should leave it; for the head is weakened by the continuous solutions of nature, as we witness in other limbs; and even when the patient's head is naturally weak, the first type of cautery is safer and better for me, and this one I use in all work, with salvation.	But these two methods of cauterization I do not consider right except on certain men; and I regard them as risky. In my opinion, to let the patient alone is preferable and safer, if it is to be. For the head is weakened when its natural continuity is broken, as we see in other parts, and particularly when the patient's head is weak by nature. In my opinion the first type of cauterization is safer and better; therefore use that and be safe.	0.83822155

of the cautery of the head When a sudden pain comes in one part of the head, and the illness extends to the eye, and the head has been purged by cleansing medicines, and the other treatments that I mentioned in the division of illnesses have been done, and all this is not effective: then make cauteries for him in two ways, that is to say with a sharp medicine and with iron.	When there occurs pain with headache in one side of the head and the pain extends to the eye; and the patient has cleared his head with purging drugs and there has been applied the other treatment that I have mentioned in the sections on diseases, but to no avail; in this disorder cauterization is of two sorts, either with caustic or with the actual cautery.	0.82707524
The cautery with sharp medicine is that you take a clove of garlic, and remove the skin and cut it a little at both ends, and then split the temple where the pain is in such a way that you can put the clove of garlic under the skin; and when it is put in, bind it and tighten it well and leave it like that for twelve hours; and then untie it and pull out the clove of garlic, and leave the wound like that for two or three days; then put on cotton dipped in butter until pus comes out.	This is the manner of cauterization with caustic: take one clove of garlic; peel it and cut both ends off; then cut open the site of the pain in the temple with a broad scalpel till there is room to contain the clove under the skin; then introduce it under the skin till it lies completely hidden. Then bind up the wound tightly over it with pads and leave for fifteen hours; then unbind it, remove the garlic, and leave the wound open for two or three days; then apply cotton wool soaked in butter till it suppurates.	0.8910179
Afterward treat it with ointment until it is healed; and if you wish, do this with some burning medicines that I have written about in the twelfth treatise on burning medicines.	Then dress with ointment till it heals. If you would rather, you may carry it out with one of the caustic drugs that I have included in the eighteenth treatise, on caustics.	0.8544863
The cautery with iron is in this manner. Heat the cautery, of which this is the shape:	The actual cauterization with iron should be done in this way: heat a cautery of this shape.	0.7073411
And it is called "closed" because the head is made in the shape of a nail where it has a little curvature, and in the middle has a small appearance; and place it on the painful spot, and hold your hand and turn the iron a little lightly, so that half the thickness of the skin is burned; and lift your hand so that the artery, which is outside, does not burn, and from which an abundance of blood flows.	It is called the claviform; the head is nailshaped in that there is a slight curvature with a small protuberance in the middle. Apply it then to the site of the pain, hold your hand steady and revolve it little by little. Let the thickness of skin burnt be about half; then remove your hand so as not to burn a subjacent artery, for thus a haemorrhage arises.	0.76668245
Afterward, put cotton in salt water, and place it on the spot for three days; then put cotton soaked in butter there, and then treat the area with ointment until it is healed; and if you want to cauterize this migraine with the cautery that is called "cultellar", use just the tip, and be careful not to cut the artery in this migraine which is not old.	Then soak some cotton wool in saline, apply to the place, and leave three days; then apply cotton wool with butter; then treat with ointment till it heals. Or if you prefer you can cauterize this migraine with the knife-edge that juts out from the cautery; but be careful not to cut an artery, specially in this kind of migraine that is non-chronic.	0.8549292

The twisting of the mouth which is curable with the cautery is of that nature which arises from phlegm, as we have already noticed in the sections on sicknesses. But cauterization is to be carefully avoided in that type which is due to dryness or spasm of the tendon.	0.7446888

When this kind of deformity has been treated with electuaries, errhines, and gargles, and your treatment is ineffective, you should use three cauterizations: one at the root of the ear; the second a little below the temple; and the third at the junction of the lips. Let your cauterization be on the opposite side to the affected side, for the relaxation will arise in the side which seems normal. The form taken by the cauterization is that you burn him a little below the frontal prominence, on a level with the upper tip of the ear, and also on the temple the length of the thumb; carry the burning to a depth of about half the thickness of the skin. of the fistula of the anus/rectum Fistulas that occur below the anus are swellings, and growths that appear near the anus outside, or in the space between the two buttocks, where there is one fistula or several; and when this swelling is old, it opens, and releases a whitish watery fluid or thin pus, and sometimes these fistulas are perforated; and they are recognized because there is a discharge or gas when the patient defecates, and perhaps a worm comes out.	Fistulae in the lower parts are a knotting and thickening of the tissues occurring externally near the anus or in the perineum to one side. A fistula may be single or multiple. When a thickening of this kind has become chronic it opens and there flows out a white watery humidity or thin pus. These fistulae may be perforating into the rectum or bowel, or non-perforating.	0.8326249

of wounds/about wounds	On the cure of wounds.	0.4729324

The things by which wounds are made are by the throwing of a stone, and the cut of a sword and knife, or of a spear or wood or arrow, or similar things; or that are made according to the places of the body, such as a wound that falls on the neck, or on the head, or the foot, or the belly, or the liver, and similar members; and I will tell you the treatment of each wound, and will begin with simple head wounds, for the remaining will come in its chapter.	Those things that cause wounds are many: a knock from a stone, a cut from a sword or a knife, a blow by a spear, stick, or arrow; and many such things. To consider wounds according to their site on the body, they are such as a wound occurring in the head, or neck, or chest, or belly, or liver, and so on. Now I am going to describe the treatment of certain wounds, so that you may take them as a standard and a guide for other wounds. I shall begin particularly with the simple wounds of the head; for mention of the compound wounds will be made at the beginning of the third book.	0.8836877
I say that when a simple wound occurs on the head, and the bone is not broken, take note if it is from the throwing of a stone or similar things, and the skin is nevertheless broken and the wound is large, and you fear the development of a hot abscess: then immediately have him bleed from the cephalic vein, especially if little blood comes from the wound, and let the bleeding be on the opposite side from the injured part; and the patient should avoid repletion from wine and rich foods.	I say then, that when there occurs a simple wound of the head, with no accompanying bone-fracture, you should look; and if it be caused by a knock from a stone or the like, and has only broken the skin, but it is a big wound and you fear lest there occur an abscess, then bleed the cephalic vein at once without delay; draw off from him blood in proportion to his vigour if there is no contra-indication to venesection; and particularly if the haemorrhage from the wound be slight. The venesection should be on the opposite side to the wound; and caution the patient against gluttony and wine and rich food.	0.9063232
And if he does not want to bleed, then let his food be light, and he should not drink wine, nor eat meat; and put on the wound, if a hot abscess develops there, cotton moistened only in rose oil, or in astringent wine; and if there is no hot abscess, then put on the wound, if it is fresh with all its blood, and it is not altered by the air, a powder, which I will describe a little later; and if the air has altered it, then put on some ointments, until it is healed.	But if from any cause venesection is prevented, he should be on a light diet and avoid meat and wines. of the wound from a lance or knife If it is a blow from a lance or knife, and the wound is deep, then see if wind comes out of the wound when the patient breathes: know then that the wound is mortal; and if it is not deep, and it is fresh with all its blood, do not put the powder in it at the first hour, and do not bind it because of the blood so that it does not remain deep inside, and return to the heart, and kill the patient; but put in an attractive ointment, and bind it twice a day; and if you do not have ointment, then put old cotton in the wound to suck out the moisture, and make the patient sleep on the wound for three days, or four, or more; so no spasm will come, nor bad heart tremors or shortness of breath; and when you see the good signs, then know that the wound is safe. of the rupture of the belly/abdomen The rupture of the belly/abdomen is large, medium, and small; from the large wound sometimes the intestine, or the sinews of the intestines, protrude, which makes their insertion difficult; and the stitching is more difficult, and sometimes the small wound is worse than the large one, because it is harder to put the intestines back in, if they are not put back in immediately; because the intestines swell as soon as they feel the cold, so they are harder to put back in, and therefore the medium wound is better, because the intestine is more easily returned to its place than in the other two. And know that the rupture of the belly/abdomen is of four kinds: the two common stitches occur in the stitching of the belly/abdomen, and the stitching of the other wounds of the body and two other specific stitches are common to other wounds; and yet they are more specific to the stitching of the belly/abdomen, and I will describe each one and explain.	On wounds of the abdomen, and protrusion of the intestine; and on suturing them. The tear that occurs in the abdomen may be great or small or it may be middle-sized. From a large tear one or more segments of gut may protrude. The reduction of this and the suture of the wound is an exceedingly hard and difficult matter.	0.7006409
I say that when the wound is small, and some part of the intestine comes out, and it is difficult to put it back, this is for one of two reasons: either because of the smallness of the wound, as we have said, or because the intestine is swollen from the cold.	I begin then by saying that when the wound is small, and a piece of intestine protrudes from it, rendering the reduction of it difficult, the difficulty will be due to one of two causes: either on account of the smallness of the fissure, as we said; or because the gut has become inflated on account of the coldness of the air.	0.8725742
And when it is cold, warm it in this way: wet a sponge or a soft cloth in lukewarm water only, or in which you have cooked squinanth, cypress, and spikenard, and sprinkle the intestine until the swelling is gone; and sometimes this makes the veins contract, and is better than plain water for reducing swelling. When the broken bone has begun to mend, the patient should be nourished with very nourishing food, fat, strong, having some glutinous property, such as porridge, rice, heads, trotters, the tripe of cows, eggs, fresh fish, heavy wine, and the like; for on this diet the mending of the fracture will be quicker and better.	0.8762227
And know that the broken bones of the old and decrepit cannot heal or knit well according to their nature because of the dryness of the limbs, nor can those who are too tender, such as the bones of small children; but it doesn't matter, replace the broken bone with a large splint which holds and joins the limb together, until it strengthens as it was before; and do not stop its work, and for this reason it must be nourished with rich foods, as we have said.	You should know that fractures occurring in mature and old people cannot join and mend into the original condition, on account of the dryness and hardness of their bones; though any bones that are extremely soft, like those of infants, may make a union and heal. Yet nature produces around the bone on all sides something like glue, with a certain viscosity, with whose help it joins, and it binds so that one part adheres to the other and they are linked to one another until it be good and firm as it had been before; so that there is no impediment to any of its functions. It is on this account that the patient's food should be the sort that has solidity and glueyness and thickness, as we have said.	0.8070723
And know that fractures are diverse in many ways.	You should know that there are as many different types of fracture as there are members.	0.61177677

For there is a fracture that goes across, and another obliquely, like the cut of a quill for writing.	Thus a fracture of the leg bone differs from a fracture of the cranium; and a fracture of the breast bone differs from a fracture of the bone of the back; and similarly there are differences among the other parts. For there is a fracture that goes across, and another obliquely, like the cut of a quill for writing. And there is a fracture with a wound, and a fracture with only a small crack. And each type has its own technique, and its own specific instrument for restoration, according to what comes to mind in its place; and the fracture of the bone is known because the place is twisted, and is clearly apparent by default, and sounds when pressed by the hand; and when there is no twisting nor sound when touched, and the patient does not feel great pain, there is no fracture; but there can indeed be twisting, or a crack, or a small fracture.	The fracture of each bone also has its own variations; for the fracture may be a clean break without splintering; or the fracture may be along the bone, with splinters and projections which may be sharp or may not; and the fracture may involve a wound and a laceration of the skin; and the fracture may be a crack. Each kind has its own special technique of setting which will be mentioned in detail, each in its place. Among the diagnostic signs of a broken bone are: distortion, protrusion, the aspect of it, and the crepitus when you press on it with the hand. of the various types of fractures	The kinds of fracture occurring in the head are many, of various forms and differing causes.	0.4952455
There are many types of fractures, and various forms, and the causes are distinct; for there are fractures that are made by the blow of a sword, and the blow removes all the bone down to the syfac which is under the bone, as an axe or wood does; and therefore this type of fracture is called "hachure" (axe-like).	Some fractures are due to a sword-stroke which may cut right through the bone and reach the membrane beneath the bone, like an adze-cut in wood, for which reason this kind of cut is called 'ascial'.	0.6689292
Or the sword cuts the bone from above without piercing, and this type of fracture is called "absolue" (complete); and the wound of these two fractures is large or small.	Or the sword may cut through part of the bone, only slicing through the surface and not reaching the depth; this kind is called an absolute avulsion. The wounds in these two fractures can be either big or small.	0.737725
And if there is a blunt percussion, or a split or a blow from a stone, or from falling on a stone, or other similar things.	There are also some fractures accompanied by smashing or crushing; such as by a blow from a stone or by falling on a stone, and the like.	0.7274651
And if there is a piercing fracture, which reaches down to the membrane which is under the bone, or it is on the bone, and this wound is either large or small.	A fracture of this kind will be either penetrating, reaching the membrane beneath the bone; or superficial; the wound in these two fractures may be either big or small.	0.7774613
And there is also a wound that is placed in the bone as fine as a hair, and the crack is small, and therefore this fracture is called "capillaire" (hairline); and also the wound which is made either by striking with a stone, or by falling, or by a similar thing, and causes the piece of bone to enter downwards, and the place is sunken as cauldrons do when struck; thus the stone enters inside.	Then there is a type of fracture that is hidden in the bone, as fine as a hair, a thin crack; therefore this kind of fracture is called 'capillary'. There is a fracture due to a fall or a blow from a stone and the like, making a dent in the surface of the bone and a hollow at the site as occurs in a bronze bowl when a blow falls on it and a portion of it is pushed in.	0.8744364
And this happens more to those who have moist bones in their head, like a child's head; and it happens in all these types of fractures that small pieces are separated like splinters; and I will speak of them in their place.	This mostly occurs in heads whose bones are soft, as those of children. Any of these kinds of fracture may also have splinters, jagged or otherwise; we shall deal with the treatment of all these in their place.	0.73595285
And we recognize all types of fractures by the indentation above them, and the probing with tents, and by the corrupted flesh that is on top.	All these kinds of fracture are diagnosed by laying them bare and investigating them with probes and removing all the lacerated tissues over them.	0.61242545
The similar hairline fracture is recognized because when the bone has been uncovered, and one rubs ink on it, the fracture appears black.	The 'capillary' kind is diagnosed by laying bare the bone, wiping it clean, and rubbing ink on it, when the fracture will appear black.	0.6154008
In the treatment of the fracture, pay attention to the patient's accidents, and if you see any bad accident, such as vomiting bile, and loss of consciousness, and stretching, and inflammation, and acute fever, and the eyes coming out and becoming red, and other similar ones in several dispositions, then it cannot heal without a doubt.	As to the treatment of the fracture: begin by paying attention to the patient's symptoms. And if you see no alarming accident, and you expect recovery, then begin the treatment.	But if you find symptoms that do not alarm you and you see hope of recovery for him, then begin treatment as follows.	0.7589262
And when the wounded first comes to you, and it is in winter, it is necessary to remove the bone before the fourteenth day, as early as you can.	If he be brought to you immediately after his injury and it be the season of winter, you should try to remove the bone, at all events before the fourteenth day.	0.78707635
And if it is in summer, hurry to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the tissue under the bone is corrupted, and the accidents that we have mentioned occur.	If it be summer you must hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the covering membrane beneath the bone and over the brain be infected, and before the above-mentioned symptoms occur.	0.80314934

And if the bone is strong and hard, you must pierce it around, before you make incisions with drills, not so deep that they pierce through the skull bone, and it should have a margin or a circle on the tip, which prevents the point from passing through the bone; and you must have several of them, so that each one comes to its place in the thickness of the bone, so that you have drills for every head; and this is the form of the three types of drills:	Let the strokes upon the chisel be gentle lest the head be shocked or injured. If the bone be strong and hard you should, before you use the chisel, make perforations round about it with drills called 'non-sinking' drills. Their name of non-sinking drills is due to their not piercing the cranial bone through to what is beneath, because the drill has a circular margin beneath its sharp head like a small collar or wheel, that prevents it from sinking in and going beyond the thickness of the bone. You must obtain a large number of these drills each of which will suit a certain thickness of bone; so that for each skull you have a drill whose pointed extremity is of a length to suit the thickness of that skull.	0.83665514
The method for piercing around the fractured bone is that you place a drill on the bone, and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone is pierced; then place the drill elsewhere a little further on, and so do all around, then cut with the incisors the space of bone that is between the holes, and do this as delicately as you can, and lightly, as we have said, until you remove the bone with your hand, or with some of the instruments that you have, such as forceps, pincers; and be careful not to touch the brain or the dura mater.	As to the manner of perforation round the fractured bone, you apply the drill to the bone and revolve it with your fingers until you know that the bone is pierced, then you transfer the drill to another place, making the distance between the perforations about the thickness of a probe. Then with the chisels cut out the bone between the perforations, using the utmost delicacy, as we have said. You will have to observe the utmost caution that neither drill nor chisel touch any part of the membrane.	0.8930565

And it does not cut on one side, and the part is sharp on both sides, so that the cutting edge is towards the bone; then strike with a small mallet on it, so that you cut the bone lightly all around, and take good care of the membrane that is underneath; if it is attached to the bone, remove it gently, for it can be removed well without harm, and without fear.	The lenticular part of it should be smooth and not for cutting, the sharp part of it being on both sides longitudinally, as you see; so that the lenticular part is resting upon the meninges while the cutting surface is on the bone. Then with a small hammer strike the tool on one side until you gently cut through the bone in a circle, in the confident knowledge that nothing untoward can happen to the membrane even though the operator be the most ignorant and cowardly of men; yes, even if he be sleepy. If any membrane stick to any part of the bone, strip it off with the lenticular tip of the chisel and free it gently from it, for it will come away without harm or danger.	0.8034829
And if the bone fracture does not penetrate to the membrane, and the bone is only cut on the surface, and there are small pieces, you must scrape this roughness, and remove the scales with a fine scraper of which you must have various sizes, to use in each place the most suitable, according to the work and the shape of the fracture.	If it be a fracture of the bone not reaching to the meninges, only the surface of the bone being separated, and there remain some roughness and fine splinters, you should smooth down the roughness and remove the splinters with fine scrapers, of which you should have a number, of varying sizes so that you can use the one best fitted and most suitable, in the light of what is suggested by your operative method and the broken bone itself and its shape.	0.8197571
So scrape first with large scrapers, and then with finer ones, and do not cease doing this until it is well done.	When you smooth the bone you must first use the broadest of the scrapers and then a finer one, and so on until you come to use the finest and slenderest of all.	0.6347195

As to the rest, the little capillary fractures and fine fissures, you will have to apply to each of them that treatment that will bring about a perfect repair. Take care then, if any bad accident happens to the patient, as we have said, it is undoubtedly doubtful.	Sometimes the surface meninges go black when the bone is removed, especially when the treatment of them is neglected; so observe, and if the patient exhibit any of the symptoms that we have mentioned you may know that he is undoubtedly doomed.	0.40313447

Know that sometimes both parts of the nose above are broken, or one of them alone, for these are two bones; and the one below is cartilaginous, and does not break, but only bends or twists, and then heals. The lower portion is cartilaginous and unbreakable; what can happen to it is contusion, twisting, or flattening.	0.8343532

Know that the shoulder can only be dislocated in three ways: one of which is that it dislocates downwards towards the armpit; and the second is towards the breast; and sometimes it dislocates upwards, but this happens rarely; and it does not dislocate backwards because of the shoulder blade, and it does not dislocate inwards because of the nerves; and it often dislocates downwards towards the armpit, especially in those who have little flesh, for in them it slips out easily; in those who have enough flesh, it dislocates with difficulty, and is reduced with difficulty.	You should know that there are three ways in which the humerus may be dislocated. One is a dislocation down into the axilla, another is a dislocation toward the breast; it may also be dislocated upward, which is rare. It is not dislocated backward, because of the scapula; nor is it dislocated forward, on account of the tendons. Mostly it is dislocated and displaced down into the axilla, and particularly in those of a spare build; in such it quickly comes out and quickly goes back.	0.8782434
And sometimes it happens to some men that they receive a blow or fracture, and the shoulder swells with a hot abscess, and they think it is dislocated because of this.	Sometimes a man suffers a blow or a fall, so that the upper arm swells up with an effusion, whence he forms the opinion that there is a dislocation.	0.76168454
You must therefore examine it, until you are certain; and then begin the treatment.	So you must examine it to make sure of it and then proceed to treat it.	0.865415
And the method is that the practitioner raises the patient's hand upwards, then puts their fists under the armpit, and forcefully lifts the joint upwards into place; and the practitioner raises the hand, and extends it upwards, then brings it downwards; it will return quickly.	To reduce it, his hand should be lifted up by an assistant, then you should put both your thumbs under his axilla and lift the joint up powerfully into its place, while the assistant lifts and stretches his hand upward; then he puts it down and the dislocation will at once go back.	0.74685353
And if it does not return as we have said within a few days, then the patient should be bathed in hot water; and the practitioner should apply softening and soothing poultices, such as a decoction of marshmallow, fenugreek, and melilot in water; then the patient should lie on their back, and a pad of wool, even, neither too soft nor too hard, should be placed under the armpit, then the practitioner puts their heel on the pad, and skillfully lifts the head of the shoulder, and pulls the patient's hand; and another practitioner holds the patient's head, so that it does not move downwards; that shoulder will return immediately, and if you wish, reduce it in this way: have a man present who is larger than the patient; and he should stand straight at the side, and put his shoulder under the patient's armpit, and lift the armpit upwards, so that the patient hangs in the air; and another practitioner pulls the patient's hand down towards the belly. And if the patient is too light, hang something heavy with him, and the dislocation will return immediately. And it can also be reduced in another way: a long stick is driven into the ground, the head of which should be as round as a pestle, and neither too thick nor too thin; then it is placed under the patient, but with soft cloths on top, and the patient lies along the length of the wood; then their hand is extended downwards strongly on the other side, and the joint will quickly return to its place; and if it does not want to return after all these things that we have said, then do this treatment: take a stick two cubits long and four fingers wide, which has a round end to be able to enter deep under the armpit, in this way:	Then the patient should lie on his back and you put under his axilla a ball of wool of moderately hard consistency. Then the doctor should put his heel on the ball and grasp the patient's hand and draw it downward, while pushing the ball with his foot, and it will straightway be reduced. Or, if you prefer, reduce it in this way: get a man taller than the patient to stand on one side and pass his shoulder under the patient's axilla and lift it up until the patient is suspended in mid-air, while another assistant pulls the patient's hand down towards his lower abdomen (if the patient is light it will be necessary to hang a weight from him); then the dislocation will straightway be reduced. It is also reduced by another method: that is, to fix in.	0.8167548
Then tie soft cloths over the end of the stick so that the stick does not injure the patient, then place it under the patient's armpit; and extend their arm along the stick, and tie the shoulder to the stick, and the arm at the end of the hand, then place the arm on a rung of a ladder lengthwise, and the hand should be extended downwards, and the body left hanging on the other side: the joint will go back in immediately.	Then you bind on the round head soft rags so that the wood will not hurt the patient. Then it is placed under the patient's axilla, and his whole hand and arm are stretched down over the spar, which is to be bound down to the upper arm, the forearm, and the tip of the hand. Then let the arm be placed cross-wise over the rung of a ladder and the hand stretched downward, and let the rest of the body hang down on the other side; then the joint will very quickly go back.	0.88646674
And when it is in, in whatever way, you must place a ball of wool evenly under the armpit, then apply the prepared plaster of mill dust, with incense and egg white, over the entire shoulder and around it, then tighten it suitably under the armpit, and wrap the bandage over the plaster, and bind the arm to their neck, and the arm should remain like this.	Once it has gone back properly, in whatever way is possible, there should be put under the axilla a ball of wool of moderate size; then apply the plaster prepared from mill-dust with olibanum and egg-white, all round the shoulder. Then firmly bandage the ball under the axilla and carry the bandaging round over the plaster.	0.80186987
And they should eat little, until the limb is strengthened, because it will heal faster; and then eat more after five or seven days, and move it; and if it is firm and not soft, then it is healed.	The patient's diet should be light until the limb gets strong, for that will hasten the cure. After five or seven days it can be unfastened and he can make trial of its movement; if it is firm and not slack then it is healed.	0.7994379
And if the joint is out of place due to moisture that has come there, or for another reason, you must apply cauteries with three holes, as described above in the chapter on cauteries.	If the joint is frequently dislocated, on account of a humidity arising in it or some other cause, then use should be made of the cautery with three prongs spoken of earlier, in the book on cauterizations.	0.77076256
And if you do all this, and it loosens after seven days, and the joint does not hold, and they cannot lift their arm up, then know that the nerves at the top of the shoulder are cut or stretched or weakened; and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.	If you do all this and loosen the bandaging after a week and the joint is still not firm, and you repeatedly plaster it and bandage it, and it is still loose and comes down and dislocates and you are unable to raise it up, then you may know that the tendons at the head of the humerus are either cut through or stretched or loosened; in which case you may know that the joint will never stay firm in its place.	0.8271177
The dislocation which is towards the chest, and the breasts, or behind, is caused by a push, and stretching of the hands, and another bandage is applied there; and treat it until it is healed.	As to the dislocation toward the chest and breast, and posteriorly, the reduction of this is done by thrusting and extension with both hands until it goes back. And apply the usual bandaging and treatment until it is healed.	0.7518904
And if after healing, stiffness comes to the limb, and slowness in movement, the patient should use baths many times, until that stiffness is relieved, and it returns to its original nature.	If, after it has healed, a callosity arises in it, and it is slow in movement, the patient should take frequent hot baths until that callosity is softened and the member returns to its original condition.	0.801806

Of the dislocation of the elbow	On the treatment of a dislocation of the elbow.	0.6050689
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty, and with difficulty returns; and it is dislocated in all directions, and especially forward and backward, and the dislocation is not hidden, for it is easily seen and felt; and dislocates according to each position. And when you compare the injured elbow with the healthy one, you can see it clearly. For the joint is locked, and he cannot bend his arm, nor touch his shoulder at all.	The elbow is with difficulty dislocated and with difficulty put back. Now dislocation may be in all directions, and particularly forward and backward. Whatever way it is dislocated it will not be concealed from you, because it is both visible and palpable, and whenever you compare the dislocated elbow with the sound one it is quite openly and plainly manifested; for the elbow joint is hollow and the patient cannot flex the forearm back or touch his shoulder with it.	0.89936143
And you must reduce it before a hot abscess develops, because if a hot abscess were to develop, it would be difficult to reduce; and perhaps it cannot be reduced completely, especially when it is backward; it is worse than other dislocations, and is more painful, and perhaps death results.	You must hasten and reduce the dislocation as rapidly as possible before an abscess occur. For if an abscess occur the reduction of it is difficult and maybe it will never be healed; especially when it is a posterior dislocation, for this is the worst and most painful of all the kinds of dislocation, and often it is fatal.	0.83201385
And the restoration, if it is such that it can be reduced, is that you extend his hand with your two hands; and his arm should be extended, and the physician's hands around the elbow, above and below, and push the joint together at the base of his thumb or at the base of his palm, until it returns to its place.	Now when it is one of those that can be reduced, the way to reduce it is for an assistant to perform a two-handed extension of the patient's hand, the patient's forearm being opened out, while the doctor's hands are above and below the elbow joint and he thrusts the joint with both thumbs together or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.	0.80682987
And if the dislocation is forward, it returns when he folds his hand over, and strikes the dislocated elbow with the base of the palm.	If it be an anterior dislocation, it may go back if the hand is bent sharply back until it strikes its own shoulder with the root of the palm.	0.7352823
And if the joint is firm in its place, untie the bandage and leave it; and if you see that the joint is not tight, reapply the plaster and the bandage, and leave it like that for a few days, until it is firmly set; then untie it.	Then undo it; and if the joint remains fixed in its place, then untie the bandaging and leave it. If you see that the joint is not thoroughly firm, replace the plaster and bandaging and leave it for a few more days until it is firm; then untie it.	0.9418758
And if he feels severe pain after the reduction of the joint, and it moves, bathe and rub it gently, and compress it until the pain subsides; or you can put a fatty sheep's caul on the joint, then leave it for a day and a night, then remove it, and have him enter the bath; and when he sweats, rub the joint temperately, then put the caul back on, and then have him enter the bath; and do this two or three times, until he is healed; and if you wish, apply warm, moist cow dung with butter, and bind it on; it will return to its normal state.	If, after the reduction and unbandaging of the joint, it becomes stiff and is difficult to move, soften it in the bath and apply gentle friction and massage until it is softened, or apply to the joint the tail of a fat-tailed sheep; bind it on and leave it on for a day and a night; then remove it and send him to the bath, and when he sweats apply moderate friction to the joint; then replace the tail and again and again, alternating with the bath, until the joint softens. If you prefer, apply to it, and bandage on, fresh cow-dung lightened with butter. Do this a number of times and it will soften and return it to its original state.	0.9123198

The dislocation of the hand.	On the treatment of a dislocation of the wrist.	0.5533913
The joint of the hand dislocates, and its reduction is easier than other joints. On the dislocation of the spine of the back	On the treatment of dislocation of the spinal vertebrae.	0.7260479
When the spine of the back or neck is dislocated, or if there are several dislocated, there is no cure, for death quickly takes the patient.	When any of the vertebrae of the back or the neck are completely dislocated or a number of vertebrae are displaced, there is no treatment in this case for death is hastening toward the patient.	0.85889876
And the sign is that the excrement comes out involuntarily, and cannot be held, and often some limbs are weakened, either both thighs or both arms or one.  And often the spine is distorted in four ways; that which is distorted backwards is called "gibbosity": the treatment is that you see if the gibbosity has come from childhood: if so there is no cure or healing at all.	The sign of this is that the injured man passes a motion involuntarily, being unable to hold it back; often too some of his limbs go limp, either both legs or both arms or one of them. If, as often happens, a single vertebra is displaced, the displacement may be in any of four directions. When there is a displacement backwards it is called 'curvature of the spine'. As to its treatment: examine it; if the curvature dates from childhood then there is no treatment or cure at all for it.	0.835485
And that which comes from a fall or blows or similar things, the ancients spoke of methods of treatment with grand words, where there is not much benefit in any of them; but I have shortened it according to my understanding, and have designed instruments in various ways.	As for the sort that occurs from a fall or a blow or the like, the Ancients indulged in lengthy dissertations thereon, giving many kinds of treatment, the greater part of which is of no use. But I have abbreviated from it, making a little which will serve in place of their much, consisting in my clarification and exposition of the sense. I have also depicted the instrument differently from their account and their explanation.	0.81476295

chapter-3-subchapter-31.txt
Know that at the joints of the hip and at the joints of the shoulder, only dislocations occur, and they do not occur as in other joints; and the hip joint is dislocated in four directions: inwards and outwards, and forwards and backwards; and more inwards than elsewhere, and rarely dislocates forwards and backwards.	You should know that a complete dislocation alone is sustained by the hip and shoulder joints; they do not sustain the lesser displacements, with concavity, as do the other joints. Now the hip joint is dislocated in four directions, interior, exterior, posterior, and anterior. Most commonly it is dislocated interiorly; and rarely is it dislocated anteriorly or posteriorly.	0.86800027
And the sign of its inward dislocation is that when the injured leg is compared with the healthy one, it is longer, and the patient's knee appears more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their knee towards the buttock; and the place nearby is swollen with a hot abscess, because the head of the femur goes there.	The indication of an internal dislocation is that when you compare the patient's sound leg with the injured leg it will be longer and the knee will be prominent as compared with that of the sound leg; nor will it be possible for the patient to flex the foot back on the groin; and there will be an obvious swelling near the groin because the head of the femur has moved there.	0.8826363
And the sign of the one that goes outwards, is that the symptoms are opposite to the previous ones.	The sign of the occurrence of an external dislocation: the symptoms will be the exact contrary to these.	0.61647105
And the signs of the one that goes forwards is that they extend their leg fully; however, they do not bend the knee without pain, and if one wants to make them walk, they cannot go forward, and they retain their urine, and the groin swells, and when they walk, they step on their heel.	The sign of an anterior dislocation is that the patient can stretch his leg out fully but cannot flex it without feeling pain in the knee; and if he tries to walk he will be unable to walk forward; and he has retention of the urine and his groin swells, and in walking he hobbles upon his heel.	0.8520026
And the signs of the one that goes backwards is that they cannot extend or bend their knee, and that leg is more crooked than the other, and the groin is softened, and the head of the femur is located beneath the ribs.	The sign of a posterior dislocation is that he cannot extend the knee, nor can he flex it before flexing the hip; also the leg will be shorter than the other, the groin will be relaxed, and the head of the femur will be evident at the lumbar region.	0.7892684
The reduction of this type of dislocation is as follows: you consider if the dislocation is old, and it remains fixed in its position, there is no cure; and one should not be presumptuous.	As to the reduction of the various forms of this dislocation, you must examine the case and if it be an old dislocation which the patient has suffered from for a long time and the reduction of which has not been contrived and which remains as it was, there is no treatment for it at all, nor should any be attempted.	0.68294096
And in the one whose dislocation is fresh, and it is of the four types of dislocation, hurry to turn the joint, and stretch it inwards and outwards, and move it right and left; perhaps it will return, and no other treatment will be necessary.	But in that case whose dislocation is recent, whichever of the four kinds of dislocation it be, hasten to rotate the joint and extend it internally and externally and move it to the right and the left; for often it will return and no further treatment will be needed.	0.8629794
And if it does not return in this manner, you must prepare a strong assistant, and strongly extend the leg with two hands, or with a strap tied above the knee; and another assistant extends their hand from above in such a way that they place their hands under the armpits, then gently tighten a strap on the thigh; and another holds the end of the strap; and they all pull upwards and downwards at the same time, until the patient is lifted from the ground all at once, and hangs suspended.	But if it does not go back thus, you must have ready a sturdy and powerful assistant to extend the leg from below, either with both his hands or with a wrapper bound upon the leg above the knee; while another assistant pulls from above by putting his hands under the patient's axillae; then you should bind a soft wrapper upon the root of the thigh, with a third assistant to hold the end of the bandage. Then extension should be made either anteriorly from the direction of the groin to the direction of the clavicle, or posteriorly toward the dorsal spine. Let them all make their extension simultaneously so the patient is lifted up bodily and remains suspended.	0.9034256
This method of extension is common to the four types, and if the dislocation has returned, as we have said, that is good.	This kind of extension is common to all four kinds of dislocation. If the dislocation goes back by the way we have said, good.	0.86997116
If not, then a treatment specific to its type must be applied. When the dislocation is inwards, you should lay the patient on their healthy side, then place the strap on the top of the thigh, and the place that is below the groin, then stretch the strap upwards towards the groin, and towards the cleft; then another assistant takes their arms, and embraces the thick part of the affected thigh, and extends it outwards strongly: it will return to its place immediately; and this type of dislocation is easier than the others.	Otherwise, each kind must have the special treatment I shall describe. When it is an internal dislocation then the special reduction is for the patient to lie upon his sound side; then apply a wrapper to the root of the hip in the space between the head of the femur and the part below the groin; then carry the bandage upward from the groin toward the upper part of the body, in the direction of the clavicle. And if you cannot reduce it in this way that we have described, you must tie the patient's two feet together with a strong, soft strap above both heels, and above both knees, and the injured leg should be extended two fingers' breadth more than the other; then it should be hung from the end of a wooden beam, and be two arm-lengths above the ground; then instruct an assistant to embrace the top of the thigh, and let another assistant hang with the patient, who firmly embraces the thigh, for the joint will return to its place quickly and immediately.	But if you find it refractory and it does not answer to this method of treatment, then the patient's two feet will have to be bound together with a strong soft bandage round his two ankles and his two knees. chapter-3-subchapter-34.txt
When the fingers or toes are dislocated, they should be gently extended without force, and this is an easy thing.	On the treatment of dislocation of the toes. Any that are dislocated must be straightened by means of gentle extension, without violence; that is not hard but positively easy.	0.6789979
